# Complete Guide to Java Design Patterns: Creational, Behavioral, and Structural

> In this course, developer Bethan Palmer guides you through using creative, behavioral, and structural design patterns in Java. Find out how design patterns help you write cleaner, more elegant code, and explore a wide range of patterns with real world examples. Practice and reinforce what you learn with hands-on challenges in each chapter. The best way to learn a language is to use it in practice.

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural) | 5h 6m | Intermediate | 16K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/improve-your-code-with-design-patterns?u=76281980&t=0)** Using [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) can make you a faster and more effective developer. If you want to write simple, maintainable, and effective code, this course will show you some shortcuts to doing just that. Knowing the different patterns and when to use them will speed up the development process and save you from reinventing the wheel. I'll share the specific details of the most well-known design patterns. I'll also discuss when to use them to improve your code. I'll teach you each pattern by using some real [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) code examples to demonstrate how they can be used. I'll also be giving you a short hands-on exercise to practice for each pattern, which you'll be able to do in [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) [Codespaces](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Codespaces.md) or in your own IDE. Then for each one, I'll walk through the solution so you can check your answers and your working. My name is Bethan Palmer, and I'm a senior Java developer and member of the Java Champions. So join me in my course to learn how to write cleaner, simpler, and more maintainable code.

### 1. Design Patterns

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What is a design pattern?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-is-a-design-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-is-a-design-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** As developers, we're constantly having to make decisions about the best way to design our code. Sometimes it can be difficult to know the best way to structure something. We want our code to be flexible and efficient and reusable, which can be tricky. Luckily, most of the time someone else has had the same problem or at least a similar one. And over time, people have come up with the best ways to solve these design problems and given them names and shared them. This means that when you're stuck on trying to work out the best way to do something, the good news is that you can reuse other people's knowledge and experience. Knowing some well-established [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) can save you from having to reinvent the wheel. Imagine someone who wants to cook lasagna. They don't have to come up with how to make it themselves. Lots of people have already made lasagnas, so instead they can follow a recipe and reuse the knowledge of someone who's already taken the time to come up with a good way to do it. Similarly, in coding, solutions to problems that have already been solved by others can be used to save time. So a design pattern is a solution to a common problem that developers have when writing code. The other good news is that learning design patterns doesn't require any knowledge of a specific technology. It doesn't matter if you're designing a brand new web application using Spring or you're working on a huge legacy desktop application or working on a gaming app. What the program does isn't important when it comes to using design patterns. Going back to the lasagna analogy, a cookbook doesn't provide you with an actual lasagna. It just gives you guidelines on how to make one. Similarly, a design pattern doesn't give you any actual code to use. It's just a guideline or template on how to solve a common problem. Some of the most well-known design patterns are described in a book
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-is-a-design-pattern?u=76281980&t=95)** called Design Patterns: Elements of Reusable Object-Oriented Software. The authors of the book are often referred to as the Gang of Four, and the book is often credited with being a milestone in how software is written. In this course, we'll cover all of these well-known design patterns. You'll learn how to use them, and just as important, recognize when to use them. Knowing this will make you a much more efficient and confident [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) developer.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) (6), [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (1)

### 2. Creational Design Patterns

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What are creational design patterns?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-are-creational-design-patterns?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-are-creational-design-patterns?u=76281980&t=0)** When we first start writing programs, it's quite common to put a lot of the logic in the class. But as applications grow, this becomes less practical. Creational [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) are a set of patterns that help you to manage this complexity. This is the first of the three types of design pattern that I'm going to be looking at in this course. As an example, say that I start creating an application to keep track of invoices for a store. In the beginning, I might just create a class called invoice, and I'd keep all my logic in that class. Then over time, I would add new classes that use my invoice class. So in lots of places in the code, I would have the words "new invoice." Then one day I might need to change how invoice objects are created. For example, I might want to change the constructor, or I might want to have different configurations for different types of invoice. This is tricky because now all my code is tightly coupled to this invoice class and how new invoice objects are created. So I have to change it everywhere. And that starts a domino effect of changes that need to be made. Creational design patterns provide various techniques for avoiding problems like this one. There are two main themes that are common to most creational design patterns. The first is that they encapsulate knowledge about which concrete class the system should use. The second is that they conceal how objects are created and put together. Abstracting this information makes the code much more flexible. The system is much less constrained by what gets created, how it gets created, and when it gets created.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) (3)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)


### 3. The Builder Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Avoiding complex constructors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/avoiding-complex-constructors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/avoiding-complex-constructors?u=76281980&t=0)** As classes grow in size, one common problem is that you end up with large constructors where you have to pass in lots of different parameters. Let's take an example of a class that represents a person. A person can have lots of different attributes. For example, they can have a first name, last name, date of birth, email address, and so on. Some of the attributes could be mandatory and others could be optional. So, for example, let's say a person has to have a first name, a last name, and a date of birth, but they don't have to have a middle name or an email address and so on. So one time when I'm creating a person object, I might want to create one with a name, a date of birth, and a middle name. But another time, I might want to create one with just the first name, last name, and an email address. And another time, I might want first name and last name, date of birth, middle name, and email. So there are lots of possible combinations I could have. Let's assume that a person object is immutable. This means that once I've set these fields when I create the person objects, I can't come back and change them later. So how might the constructor of a person object look in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) code? It might look something like this with a long list of things that I need to pass into the constructor, and for the optional ones that I don't want, I'd have to pass in null. There are a few problems with this. It's quite messy and it's easy to get things in the wrong order. If we look at an example of how this code looks when the constructor is called, it's almost impossible to know what things are being passed in, and it would be really easy to pass the wrong parameter in the wrong place. So what's the alternative? One other option is to create different constructors for all the different combinations, but this isn't practical either.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/avoiding-complex-constructors?u=76281980&t=94)** You'd end up with loads of different constructors, one for each combination, and that would be really hard to read. It's also might not be possible if some of the arguments have the same types. Java won't know the difference between two different constructors that pass in three different strings, for example. The solution to this problem is the builder pattern. In this pattern, you have a separate class that's used to construct the object using just the fields that you want, and this class is called the builder. In the traditional pattern, there's an interface for the builder and then a concrete implementation. The product is the class that would otherwise have the big constructor. So in this example, it would be the person class. And the director is the class that uses the builder to create the object. So, for example, the class where you'd be creating a new person object. In Java, is normally possible to simplify this pattern so you only have the concrete builder without the interface. So let's see how this looks with a real example in Java.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (4)
> **Analogies:** for example (4)
> **Definitions:** is called (2), means that (1)

#### [Recognize where to use the Builder pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's have a look at an example of an application that would benefit from using the builder pattern. So in this example, there's a class called person, and this has six different fields. So first name, middle name, last name, date of birth, email address, and phone number. And this is what the constructor for the person class looks like. So I'm passing in six different things, and then I'm setting each of those things to the six fields. And then there's also a check to see if first name or last name is null. And if either of them are null, then it throws an illegal argument exception, and there's a message saying, "First name and last name must not be null." So this means that first name and last name are required fields, but everything else is optional. So for all of the others, you could pass in null, if you wanted to. The only other thing in this class is a two-string method which just prints out the details of the person object. So let's see what it looks like when we use this person class. So here's a class called SalesLeadTracker. And this class has a method called addPersonToLeadTracker. So it takes a person as an argument, and then there's a message that says, "A new lead was added to the lead tracker." And then it just prints out the person's details. And above this, there's a main method. And in here, I'm creating two people. So person1 who has a first name, a last name, a date of birth, and an email, and then I'm adding person1 to the lead tracker. And then there's a person2 which has a first name, middle name, last name, and a phone number. And then I'm also adding person2 to the lead tracker. So as you can see, it's a bit difficult to see which
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980&t=93)** fields are which in this constructor. So in [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) [Codespaces](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Codespaces.md), I'm hopefully given this hint that says that this is the middle name. But if your IDE doesn't have this feature, you might not know what this is supposed to be. So it's quite hard to tell from just the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "null" that this is supposed to be a middle name, and it would be quite easy to pass in something like a last name here instead. But let's just run this and see what happens. And in the console, it prints out two messages. One that says, "A new lead was added to the lead tracker," and it prints out person1's details. And then again, "A new lead was added to the lead tracker," and then it prints out person2's details. So this works fine, but it's just quite difficult to read and to maintain. And although there's only six fields at the moment, you could see that this could become larger over time. So people could also have an address, an ID number, and things like that, which would make it even harder to read and maintain. So this is a classic example of an application that would benefit from being refactored to use the builder pattern.

#### [Improving the Builder pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/improving-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/improving-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** In this example application, I now have a fully working builder pattern. #### [Solution: The Builder pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-builder-pattern?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's have a look at my solution to this builder pattern exercise. ### 4. The Singleton Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [When to use the Singleton pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/when-to-use-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/when-to-use-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The singleton pattern is probably one of the most well-known [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md). It's also sometimes known as an anti-pattern because there are some issues which can be caused by using it. But when used correctly, there are situations where it is useful. So I'll talk about some of the common pitfalls with this pattern and how you can avoid them. But firstly, what is the singleton pattern? Normally, when you create a class in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md), you expect to instantiate many different objects of that class. For example, if you have a class called car, you'd probably have several places in your code where you create new car objects, but with a singleton pattern you prevent this from happening. You make sure there can only ever be one instance of that class. For example, only ever one car object. The next question is: Why would you want to do this? Say you have a resource that's shared across your application like a database. You might have a class that controls access to the database, and in that case, you would only want one object of that class. Otherwise, you could have a situation where multiple objects are trying to write to the same table using different instances of your DatabaseAccessor class. So some examples of where it might make sense to use the singleton pattern are controlling the connection to a database or access to configuration files or other [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) resources, such as a printer. And one of the most well-known use cases is for loggers. One of the things to consider when implementing this pattern is thread safety. If you have multiple threads in your application, each one could create a new instance of the singleton, which defeats the point. So this is something I'll show you how to handle later in this chapter. There's also the question of if you should use singletons at all.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/when-to-use-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=94)** Often there are better alternatives, and refactoring your code might be a nicer solution. One of the reasons that people don't like the singleton pattern is that an [object-oriented programming](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Object-Oriented%20Programming%20(OOP).md), a class should only do one thing. But with a singleton class, it does two things. It does whatever logic the class is supposed to do, for example, control access to a database, and it also ensures only one instance is created. Singletons also make your codes difficult to test, because the objects that use the singleton are tightly coupled to the singleton objects. So sometimes you can use other solutions, for example, [Dependency Injection](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Dependency%20Injection.md) instead of using the singleton pattern. However, if you do have a use case where you want to have only one single instance of a class, the singleton pattern will allow you to do this.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) (1), [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (1), [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) (1), [Object-oriented programming](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Object-Oriented%20Programming%20(OOP).md) (1), [Dependency Injection](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Dependency%20Injection.md) (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (4), such as (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (3)
> **Definitions:** known as (1)

#### [Implementing the Singleton pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implementing-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implementing-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's have a look at how to implement the singleton pattern in an existing application. So in this example, there's a class called PrintSpooler. And this is used to connect to a printer and print things out. So in real life it would be a bit more complex than this. But just to keep things simple, this just has a method called print which prints out the words "Pinting." But the idea is that there should only be one PrintSpooler object which controls access to the printer with this print method. So at the moment, this class is used in two different places. Firstly, there's another class called DocumentPrinter and this has a method called printDocuments which creates a new PrintSpooler object and then calls spooler.print. And then secondly, there's a class called ImagePrinter, which has a method called printImage. And this also creates a new PrintSpooler object and then calls spooler.print. So I want to make it impossible to have this new PrintSpooler call, because I don't want the DocumentPrinter and the ImagePrinter both to create separate PrintSpooler objects. So I'm going to go back to the PrintSpooler class, and I'm going to implement the singleton pattern here. So the first thing I'm going to do is I'm going to make this a final class. So after public and before class, I'm going to put final. And this means that the class can't be extended. And next, I'm going to create a private constructor. So normally in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) if you don't have any constructor, it just creates a public one for you. So I explicitly need to create a private one. And this means that I won't be able to have any new PrintSpoolers anywhere else in the code.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implementing-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=93)** It can only be created from inside this class itself. So I'm going to create my private constructor and just say "private PrintSpooler." And then it's just going to be an empty constructor. So I still need a way to create the one single instance that's going to be used, which is going to be done inside this class. So underneath my constructor, I'm going to create a field and it's going to be a private static field. And it's going to be static because I only want one instance to be created. And it's going to be a PrintSpooler object and I'm going to call it "INSTANCE." And because it's static, I'm going to put it all in capitals. So now I need a way to access this instance. So I'm going to create a new method underneath this field. And it's going to be a public method. And it's going to be a static method. So static methods are methods that belong to the class, which means you can call them without creating instances of the class, which is essential for me here, because I won't be able to create new instances of this class. So it's going to be a public static method, which is going to return the PrintSpooler INSTANCE, and I'm going to call the method getInstance. So the first thing I'm going to do in this method is I'm going to check if the instance has been instantiated already or not. So I'm going to say, if INSTANCE is equal to null, then that means this is the first time this method has been called. So I need to create a new INSTANCE object. So here I'm going to say, "INSTANCE equals new PrintSpooler." And I don't need an else statement because if the instance has
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implementing-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=187)** already been initialized, then I don't need to do anything else. All I need to do now is return the instance. So underneath the if statement, I'm just going to say, "return INSTANCE." So now all I need to do is update my DocumentPrinter and ImagePrinter classes. So I can see inside my DocumentPrinter class that it no longer compiles where I have new PrintSpooler, which is good. So I'm going to get rid of that, and I'm going to replace it with my static method. So I'm going to say, var spooler equals PrintSpooler.getInstance. Then I'll do the same in ImagePrinter. So again, I'm going to get rid of the new PrintSpooler and say instead PrintSpooler.getInstance. So now I've implemented the singleton pattern in this application. However, this current solution isn't thread-safe. So in the next video, I'm going to show another solution which is thread-safe.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** instance (6)
> **Code Identifiers:** getinstance (3), printdocuments (1), printimage (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)

#### [Thread safety with the Singleton pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/thread-safety-with-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/thread-safety-with-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** A common pitfall when implementing the singleton pattern is not making the solution thread-safe. So, for example, in this PrintSpooler class, you can imagine a scenario where two threads are using this application, and they both call this getInstance method at the same time. And then they both check if INSTANCE is equal to null at the same time. And now the thread has actually created this new PrintSpooler yet. So in both cases, this is true. So they both come in here and they both create a new PrintSpooler objects. So then I've ended up with two PrintSpoolers when I should only ever be able to create one. So one easy way to solve this issue would be to make this method a synchronized method. And this means that only one thread at a time would be able to come inside this method. But one thing to be cautious of is that if you're expecting that this method is going to be called lots of times, it can slow down the execution of the program. So another solution to this problem is to make this class into an enum. So the first thing I'm going to do is where it says "public final class PrintSpooler" is I'm going to get rid of final class and change that to enum. Then, instead of my instance being a field, is going to be an enum value. So I'm just going to get rid of the private static PrintSpooler and just leave the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "INSTANCE." So that's a value of the enum. And then the next thing to do is inside this getInstance method is I can actually remove this whole if statement. So I'm just left with return INSTANCE. So this will create an instance of the class when the enum is first used, and the next time it will just use the existing one, which is why I can get rid of that if statement.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/thread-safety-with-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=95)** So it basically does what I had before, but with less code. And the nice thing is that enums in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) are guaranteed to always be thread-safe. So this is a nice way of making sure it definitely works as I intended without having to implement that thread-safe guarantee myself. And it's actually a simpler solution to the one I had before with less code. One thing to note is that you wouldn't be able to use this approach if the singleton class has to implement a superclass, for example. But otherwise, this is probably the better approach for implementing the singleton pattern.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) (1), [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** instance (3)
> **Analogies:** for example (2), imagine (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Code Identifiers:** getinstance (2)
> **Definitions:** means that (1), is a  (1)
> **Warnings:** pitfall (1)

#### [Challenge: The Singleton pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/challenge-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/challenge-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=5)** Now, it's time to practice implementing the singleton pattern in a short challenge. So in this example application, there's a class called Logger. And this has a single method called log which takes a string as an argument and then it just prints out that message. So in a real world, this would probably be a bit more complex. But the idea here is to practice the singleton pattern rather than learn how loggers work internally. So there's a simple Logger class, and then there are two more classes that use it called Cat and Dog. So, for example, if I look inside the Dog class, there's a field called Logger where it creates a new Logger object. And then there's a woof method which logs the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "Woof." And then the Cat class is exactly the same, but the method is called meow, and it logs the word "Meow" instead of Woof. And then finally, there's a main class with a main method inside it. So this creates a new Dog object and then calls dog.woof, and then a Cat object and calls cat.meow. So if I run this, I can see in the console that it prints out Woof and Meow. So the purpose of this exercise is to refactor this Logger class to make it into a singleton. So have a go at doing it from memory, and if you get stuck, you can always see the example in the previous video for a reminder about how to implement this pattern. And I'll also be going through the solution to this exercise in the next video as well. So good luck and have fun!

#### [Solution: The Singleton pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's have a look at a solution to this singleton pattern exercise. So the first thing I'm going to do is I'm going to change this Logger class into an enum. So I'm just going to change the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "class" to enum. And then I need a value, which is going to be the instance of my logger. So I'm going to call it "INSTANCE." So just all in capitals, the word "INSTANCE," followed by a semicolon. And next, I'm going to create a method to get hold of this instance. So I'm going to say, public static, and it's going to return a logger. And I'm going to call it "getInstance." Then I can just say, "return INSTANCE." So that's all I need to do in the Logger class. So now I just need to update my Dog and Cat classes. So let's start with the Dog class. And instead of new Logger, I'm going to get rid of that, and I'm going to say, "Logger.getInstance." And actually, another thing you can do is you can just say, "Logger.INSTANCE." So you don't actually need the getInstance method at all, but it's nice to have it explicit. So either way, is completely fine. And then the same in the Cat class. I'm going to change new Logger to Logger.getInstance. One thing I didn't do is I didn't create a constructor in the Logger class. And this is because for enums, loggers are actually private by default. But if you did create a private constructor, that's great because it makes it really explicit. So that's one way to implement the singleton pattern in the Logger class. So you might have done it differently,
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-singleton-pattern?u=76281980&t=97)** and you might have created a normal class instead of an enum. And that also works completely fine.

### 5. The Prototype Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Prototype pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Imagine a scenario where you have to create lots of very similar objects. As an example, say you're creating a user interface which displays a forest of trees. To create the display, you'd need to create lots of Tree objects. For example, say you need to create 50 fir trees, 50 oak trees, and 50 silver birch trees to create the scene. Let's think about how this might look when it's implemented in code. The class structure might look something like this with an interface at the top called Graphic. And then there might be a class that extends this called Tree, which has a constructor that takes the type of tree and the heights. And the code to create all the Tree objects would then be a long list of new Tree variables, where we pass in lots of the same information repeatedly. So I do this for all the fir trees, which are the same height, and then I need to do the same for all the oak trees, which are also the same height as each other. One way to help solve this repetitiveness would be to add a new layer to the class hierarchy. So below Graphic, you have Tree, and below Tree, you would have the different types of Tree. This means I wouldn't have to repetitively set the type and the heights of all the new Tree objects I created, because the fir tree would already have the height set and oak tree and silver birch tree and so on. However, one drawback to this is that it makes the hierarchy more complex, especially if I start adding new types of trees later. So the other option is to use the prototype pattern. With this pattern, the interface at the top of the hierarchy defines a clone method, and then the Tree class also has a clone method. This clone method means that we can copy the configuration of an existing objects without having to complicate the class hierarchy. Think of Dolly, the sheep that was cloned.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980&t=94)** The prototype pattern essentially does this for [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) objects, so that similar objects don't have to be configured multiple times. One thing to note is that there is an interface called Cloneable in Java. One way to implement this pattern is to use this Cloneable interface. However, there are some issues with this interface that is worth knowing about. Firstly, it has no clone method, so using it doesn't really tell us much about what the implementing class is going to do, and it's also a bit of a hassle to implement. We have to implement the interface and handle the exception it throws without any real benefits. So for these reasons, the Cloneable interface is often avoided in Java. In my examples, I'm going to be using an approach called the copy constructor, which is a bit simpler to implement.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (3)
> **Definitions:** means that (1), is an  (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1), for example (1)

#### [Implement the Prototype pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's see how the prototype pattern works with a real example. This is an app that keeps track of orders placed at a restaurant. So the first class we're going to look at is a class called Meal. This is an abstract class which has a single fields of type int called price, and it has a simple constructor that sets the price, and then a getter and a setter for the price. And so far, there's one class that extends this abstract class and that's the Pizza class. So a pizza is a type of meal. So it has a price, and it also has this extra field called PizzaFlavor. And this class just has a constructor where you pass in the price and flavor and then a getter and setter for the flavor and then a two-string method to print out the Pizza object in a nice way. So let's have a look at this PizzaFlavor class. This is a class that has a field called Name, which is a string, and this is passed into the constructor and set there. And then again, there's a getter and a setter and a two-string method. So there's an abstract class called Meal and then a concrete implementation which is Pizza. And then Pizza has a PizzaFlavor, which is just the name of the flavor of the pizza. There's one final class which is the OrderTracker class. So this has a main method where I'm creating all of the different pizzas for the order. So first of all, it creates a pizza flavor called Hawaiian. And then it creates five new pizzas with the price of four and the flavor "Hawaiian." Then I do the same with pepperoni. So I'm creating a pizza flavor called pepperoni, and then five pepperoni pizzas with the price of five and the flavor "pepperoni."
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980&t=94)** Then I'm adding all of the pizzas to a list, and then I'm calling a method called placeOrder, which just prints out a message saying "Order placed," and then it prints out all of the items in the list. So let's just run this quickly to see what that looks like. So here, I've got a list of all my 10 pizzas. So as you can see, there's a lot of duplicated efforts here. For each of the Hawaiian pizzas, I have to set the price and the flavor the same way five times, and then, again, for the pepperoni pizza below that. So this is where the prototype pattern can help to remove this duplication. So the first thing I'm going to do to implement this pattern is I'm going to go back to the Meal class, which is the abstract class. And in here, I'm going to define a clone method. So I'm just going to scroll to the bottom and create a public abstract method called clone. And it's going to return a Meal object. And it's a common convention to call the method clone when using the prototype pattern. So now I need to go to my Pizza class, and because it extends Meal, it doesn't compile anymore because I haven't implemented the clone method yet. So I'm going to scroll to the bottom of this class and add that clone method. So I'm going to add the annotation "Override" and then I'm going to say "public" and this is going to return a Pizza object. So a pizza is a type of meal. So I can return this type here, and I'm going to call it "clone." And then inside this method, I'm going to return a new Pizza object, but using the fields from the current object. So I'm going to say "return new Pizza."
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980&t=189)** And I'm going to pass in this.getPrice and this.getFlavor.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-prototype-pattern?u=76281980&t=199)** So that will create a new pizza, but with the fields set from the current objects. So now I just need to update my OrderTracker class. So I'm going to create hawaiianPizza1 in the same way as before. But then for the others, I'm going to clone it. So for hawaiianPizza2, I'm going to get rid of the new Pizza part and I'm going to say, "hawaiianPizza1.clone." And then I'm just going to copy that for all of the other ones. So for Pizza3, I'm going to call clone again. And then for Pizza4, the same again, and the same again for Pizza5. Then I'm going to do the same with the pepperoni pizzas. So for pepperoniPizza1, I'm going to create my pizza with the correct configuration. And then for pepperoniPizza2, I'm going to say, "pepperonipizza1.clone." And then, again, I'm going to copy that for Pizza3, Pizza4, and for Pizza5. And just to check it still works as before, I'm going to run this again. And I can see I still get my list of five Hawaiian pizzas and five pepperoni pizzas. So these clones Pizza objects are actually still their own separate [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) objects, but they have the same field sets as the original one. So this is a simple implementation of the prototype pattern.

#### [Handling mutability](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/handling-mutability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/handling-mutability?u=76281980&t=0)** In this example app, I'm using the prototype pattern to remove duplication when creating pizza orders in a restaurant. So I'm using this clone method on the Pizza class to clone the original pizza. However, this clone method I've implemented only creates a shallow copy of the Pizza object. I've assumed that none of the pizzas fields are going to change, so let's have a look at one of the drawbacks of this approach. So in this OrderTracker class, I've created a Hawaiian pizza and cloned it several times. But let's say that later on I want to change the flavor of the first pizza after I've cloned it. So underneath all of these clones, I'm going to change the Hawaiian pizza flavor. So I'm going to say, hawaiian.setName, and now I want to call it Hawaiian with extra cheese. So let's see what happens when I run the app now. And I can see in the output that it's actually changed the flavor for all of the clones pizzas, as well as for the original pizza. This is because the PizzaFlavor field for each cloned pizza is the same PizzaFlavor object, and if that's the functionality that you want, then everything is fine and you don't need to change anything. But if you want to avoid accidentally updating all of the cloned objects, then a deep copy needs to be made instead of a shallow copy. So to fix this, I need to change my clone method. So I'm going to go back to my Pizza class. And I'm going to scroll down to the bottom where I have my clone method implemented. So in here, I'm returning a new Pizza and I'm passing in this.getPrice and this.getFlavor, which means that I'm actually
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/handling-mutability?u=76281980&t=96)** passing in the original flavor objects. So I need to change this.getFlavor. So I'm going to remove that, and instead, I'm going to create a new pizza flavor object. So I'm going to say, new PizzaFlavor, and then inside here, I'm going to pass in this.getFlavor and then .getName. So I'm essentially cloning the pizza flavor as well as the pizza. So now if I go back to the OrderTracker and I run this again, I can see that this time the flavor has only updated for the first pizza, and the clone ones still have the original flavor. This is because the flavor field is now a separate object, so it can't be changed by accident any longer.

### 6. The Factory Method

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Factory Method pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The factory method is a pattern that handles deciding which type of concrete class needs to be created by another class. This is best explains with an example. So imagine there's an application called a ticket machine, which creates tickets for public transport. Initially, the ticket machine might only create bus tickets, so that's fairly straightforward to implement. I would have a TicketMachine class and a Ticket class, and the TicketMachine class creates Ticket objects. So far so good. But later, I might want to have ticket machines that sell different types of tickets, for example, train tickets. So in my code, I might introduce a ticket interface with two concrete implementations called bus ticket and train tickets. The problem is that the TicketMachine class doesn't know in advance which type of ticket it needs to create; bus tickets or train tickets. This is the problem that the factory method pattern solves. There are a couple of different variations of this pattern. One variation is to have a factory method inside the creator. So in this case, the TicketMachine would have a createTicket methods that can return different types of tickets. So in this createTicket method, you would pass in a parameter that would be either bus or train, and then it would return the correct concrete type based on that parameter. The alternative is to create different types of ticket machine that are responsible for creating different types of tickets. So bus ticket machines could create bus tickets and train ticket machines could create train tickets. Both approaches work well, and you can use either depending on your use case and your preference. In the language of the factory method pattern, the TicketMachine classes are the factories and the tickets are the [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) that they're creating.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980&t=92)** So to summarize, you use the factoring method pattern when you have a class that's responsible for creating objects of another type of class, but that class doesn't know in advance which concrete types of the class it should create. A nice side effect of this pattern is that the creation of the object is encapsulated in one place, so if it changes, the call to new is only in that one place and only needs to be changed there.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** createticket (2)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1), for example (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### [Implement the Factory Method pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** This is an example of an application where the factory method pattern might come in useful. It's an app for selling different types of tickets. So let's have a look at the classes we've got at the moment. So the first one is a class called Tickets. And this is actually an interface which defines a single method called getPrice. At the moment, there's only one class that implements this interface which is the BusTicket class. So this overrides the getPrice method and returns the value three. Next, there's a class called TicketMachine. And this has a method called createTicket, which returns a new BusTicket. So this is all fine if I have an app that wants to use the ticket machine to print bus tickets. So to represent that app, I have another class here called TicketOffice. And this has a field called ticketMachine where I'm creating a new TicketMachine. Then it has a method called buyABusTicket. So it creates a new ticket using the ticketMachines method to create tickets. And then it prints out a message saying, "One bus ticket purchased. The price is" and then it uses the ticket.getPrice to get the price. So if I run this, I can see in the output it says, "One bus ticket purchased. The price is $3." So what if I want to be able to buy train tickets from this ticket [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md)? So let's see how that would work. So the first thing I'm going to do is I'm going to create a new class, and it's also going to implement the ticket interface. So I'm going to create a new file called TrainTicket.[Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md). So after TrainTicket, I'm going to add implement Ticket. So I need to override the getPrice method.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980&t=96)** So I'm going to put @Override, and then it's going to be public int getPrice. And let's say train tickets are $7, so I'm going to say, "return 7." So now the ticket machine has to be able to return either a bus ticket or a train ticket. But it doesn't know in advance which one it should return. So I'm going to make this createTicket method into a factory methods. So now it's going to take an argument which is going to be either bus or train. So I'm going to use a string and I'm going to call it ticketType. Then inside this method, I'm going to check if it's bus or train. So I'm going to say, "if ticketType.equals," and I'm actually going to use .equalsIgnoreCase just in case. And I'm going to pass in bus. Then in that case, I want to return a new BusTicket. So I'm going to cut that and paste that inside the if statement. Then the only other type of ticket at the moment is a train ticket, so I can just add else. And then I'm going to say, "return new TrainTicket." And I'm missing a brackets up there. So now I need to go back to my TicketOffice class. And inside this createTicket method, I now need to pass in the type. So I want it to be a bus ticket. So I'm going to pass in bus. And now I can also create a method to buy train tickets as well. So I'm going to copy this buyABusTicket method and just paste it underneath. And then I'm going to call it "buyATrainTicket" instead of buyABusTicket.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-factory-method-pattern?u=76281980&t=188)** And when I'm calling createTicket, instead of passing in Bus, I'm going to pass in the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "Train." And let's call that inside the main method as well, just to check it works. So underneath buyABusTicket, I'm going to add buyATrainTicket. So if I run this now, I can see it says, "One bus ticket purchased. The price is $3." And then it says the same again, "One bus ticket purchased. The price is $7." So I need to update where it says "bus" to train inside the TrainTicket method. And if I run it again, this time it works as expected. So it says, "One bus ticket purchased. The price is $3." And "One train ticket purchased. The price is $7." So if I add more ticket types, I just need to update the createTicket method inside my TicketMachine class. So this is how the factory method pattern can be used to decide which concrete classes to return in a flexible and extensible way.

#### [Improve the Factory Method pattern with functional programming](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/improve-the-factory-method-with-functional-programming?u=76281980)

### 7. Abstract Factories

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand abstract factories](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-abstract-factories?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-abstract-factories?u=76281980&t=0)** Sometimes we have frameworks that use groups of classes together, and there might be different flavors of the framework that should be available for the clients using it to choose from. This framework would be made up of a family of classes. So to explain this with an example, imagine a user interface that can come in different color schemes. The framework to render the interface is made up of a family of objects. For example, there would be an interface called button and then concrete classes for each different color, and then the same for other parts of the interface, for example, scrollbars and toolbars and so on. The user interface should be able to return the right objects to create the right color scheme. If the client wants a blue application, it should return a blue button, a blue toolbar, a blue scrollbar, and so on. There are a few constraints that need to be enforced to make this work. The first is that the system should be independent of how objects are created, so the user interface shouldn't be coupled to things like how button objects are created. The second is that it should be configured with one of multiple families of objects. So, for example, the user interface should either use groups of blue objects or groups of red objects. Thirdly, the family of related objects should all be used together, so they should either be all blue or all red. If you have a group of objects with different variations that should be used together like this, the abstract factory pattern is a good way to go. The class diagram for this pattern can look quite complex, mainly because there are so many classes involved. So let's break it down step by step and see how it would work with this user interface example. First of all, you'd have an interface that defines a factory for creating a user interface, or UI for short, with a specific
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-abstract-factories?u=76281980&t=97)** color scheme. Then you'd have concrete implementations for each color, so you'd have a blue UI factory, a red UI factory, and so on. These would be responsible for creating all the different elements of the UI. So, for example, the blue UI factory would create a blue button and a blue toolbar and so on. And the red UI factory would return the red versions of those. The next part of the pattern is to have a factory that the overall application can use to determine which of these color scheme factories to use. The application composite the color scheme at once and the factory will return the right one. So if the application passes in red, it will use the red UI factory, and if it passes in blue, it will use the blue UI factory. So this pattern is essentially a factory of factories. As you can see, one drawback is that there are a lot of classes in this pattern implementation which can make it a bit hard to keep track of. And each time I add a new color or a new UI element, there are lots of new classes to create. However, the positive is, this means that the application itself doesn't need to know anything about all the different families and how the objects are created behind the scenes. It only needs to know about the user interface factory and everything else is hidden, and in turn, the user interface factory only needs to know about its concrete implementations. They handle the actual creation of the UI objects like buttons and so on. So a lot of the complexity is abstracted away. Another nice thing about this pattern is that each part of it is quite easy to unit test. Concrete classes are injected as dependencies, which means they can easily be mocked.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Analogies:** for example (4), imagine (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), means that (1)

#### [Recognize where to use abstract factories](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-abstract-factories?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-abstract-factories?u=76281980&t=0)** Let's start by looking at an application that doesn't use the abstract builder pattern, and then we can see how it can be improved. So this is an application that's used to represent the creation of different types of bicycle by a bike manufacturer. So the first class I'm going to look at is the Bike class. So this is a class that has four different fields handlebars, pedals, frontTire, and backTire. And these all set by passing the values into the constructor. And then there are getter methods for each of these fields and a two-string method. So as there are different kinds of bike that can be built, each of these fields is actually an interface. So, for example, if I look at the Handlebars class first, this is an interface with a single method called getType. And then there are two different implementations of this interface. So one of them is the MountainBikeHandlebars, and this returns the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "FLAT." So mountain bikes have flat handlebars. And then the other one is the RoadBikeHandlebars which returns the word "DROP." And then it's the same for the other fields in this Bike class. So if I look at pedals, this is another interface, and again there are MountainBikePedals. So this returns SPD which is the type of pedal used by mountain bikes. And then there's also RoadBikePedals which returns SPD-SL, which is a type of pedal used by road bikes. And then the other two fields are of type tire. So Tire is another interface with a method called getWidth. And then, again, there's two different implementations. So there's the MountainBikeTire which returns 29 for the width. And then there's the RoadBikeTire which returns
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-abstract-factories?u=76281980&t=95)** 23 for the width. So that's the general structure of the application. So if you find that you have a structure similar to this, where you have an object with lots of fields that can be of different types, that might be a sign that your application might benefit from using the abstract builder pattern. And the final class that I haven't looked at yet is the BikeManufacturer class. So this is a class with the main method in it. And this has a method called buildBike. So this takes a string which is the bikeType. And if the word "ROAD" is passed in, it creates a new bike and it passes in RoadBikeHandlebars, RoadBikePedals, and RoadBikeTire for the front tire and back tire. And then if MOUNTAIN is passed in, then it returns a new bike, but with MountainBikeHandlebars, MountainBikePedals, and MountainBikeTires. And if something else is passed in, then it throws a new IllegalArgumentException. So this is a kind of factory method for creating Bike objects. And then in the main method, I'm using the buildBike method, and first passing in the word "ROAD" and then the word "MOUNTAIN." So if I run this, I can see at the bottom there that it prints out the first bike, and this has all the features of a road bike. And then the second one has all the features of a mountain bike. So this works fine, but it's not a great design. So if I have a look again at this buildBike method, this method is tightly coupled to all the different bike parts. So if I added a new type of bike later, for example a hybrid bike, I'd have to remember to update this method.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-abstract-factories?u=76281980&t=189)** Or if one of the constructors changes for any of the bike parts, I'd have to change it in all of the different places it's used in this method. So, for example, if I now need to pass in an argument for the RoadBikeHandlebars constructor, I'd have to change it in the first if statement and in the second if statement and in all the other if statements if I add new bike types later. Or if I add a new bike part, for example, I'd again have to update every if statements. So there's some annoying type coupling here. It would also be quite easy to make a mistake. And, for example, I could pass in a mountain bike tire for a road bike, for example. So if you find yourself doing something like this to construct different families of objects depending on a type, it's worth considering using abstract factories to reduce the coupling.

#### [Implement the complete Abstract Factory pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-complete-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-complete-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Now, I have an application that uses factories for creating different types of bikes. So, for example, if I want to create a road bike, I can use my RoadBikeFactory class, and if I want to create a mountain bike, I can use my MountainBikeFactory class. However, in this BikeManufacturer class, I still have lots of boilerplate code for creating the right type of bike factory depending on the bike type. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to create another factory to handle that logic. So I'm going to create a new class, and I'm going to call this one FactoryMaker. So in here, I'm going to implement a factory method to return the right type of bike factory. So the first thing I'm going to do is I'm going to create a Map to store the bike types that I have. So I'm going to say "private static final Map," and the key is going to be a string. And the value is going to be a Supplier, which is going to return a type of BikeFactory. And I'm going to call this Map "bikeTypes." And then I'm just going to say "equals new HashMap." And then I'm just going to make sure that I've imported the Map class and the Supplier class. So then I need to populate the Map with my different types of bike. So I'm going to create a static block. So I'm going to say "static" and then a pair of curly braces.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-complete-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980&t=93)** And then in here, I'm going to say "bikeTypes.put." And I'm going to put for the key for the first one the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "ROAD." And then a supplier is basically a piece of code that's going to return an object for me. So I'm going to say "RoadBikeFactory::new." Then I'm just going to do the same for mountain bikes. So underneath that, I'm going to say "bikeTypes.put." And this time, I'm going to pass in the word "MOUNTAIN" for the key. And then for the value, this supplier is going to be MountainBikeFactory::new. And then finally, I need to create my factory method to create the right bike type. So in here, I'm going to create a new method. And it's going to be a public static method that returns a bike factory, and it's going to be called createFactory. I'm just going to take a string argument, which is going to be the bike type. So I'm going to call that bikeType. Then in here, I'm going to check if that bikeType is inside my Map. So first of all, I'm going to say "bikeTypes.get" and then pass in the bikeType. And then I'm going to check that that's not equal to null. So if it is in the Map, then I'm going to return the value for that type. So I'm going to say "return bikeTypes.get" and pass in the bikeType again.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-complete-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980&t=186)** And then I need to call .get to get the value from the Supplier. And then if it's not in there, then I'm just going to throw an illegal argument exception. So I'm going to say else and then throw new IllegalArgumentException. And as a message inside the exception, I'm going to say "Bike type not supported." So what this method is doing is it's saying if this bike type is inside the Map that I populated here, then get the value from that key and the value will actually create a new object of that type for me. So there is quite a bit going on here. And if you want to learn in more detail about how this works, I go through this step-by-step in the Factory Method chapter in more detail. So now in the BikeManufacturer class, I can really simplify this big method. So I'm going to create the right type of factory based on the type that's passed in using my new FactoryMaker. So at the top, I'm going to add a new line and say "var bikeFactory equals FactoryMaker.createFactory" and pass in the bikeType. And this is a static method, so I don't need to create a new FactoryMaker. I can just call the createFactory method directly. And then for each bike parts, I can use that bikeFactory. So actually, I can copy everything from the first if statement where I'm creating all the different bike parts, and I'm going to paste that up here and just format that so it's a bit easier to read.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-complete-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980&t=278)** And then instead of using the roadBikeFactory, I'm going to use this bikeFactory variable. So I'm going to replace everywhere where it says roadBikeFactory with bikeFactory. And then I can also copy this line where I creates the new bike. So where it says "return new Bike," I'm going to copy that and put that at the top here. And then I can actually get rid of this whole if-else block. So now I'm creating the right type of factory using my createFactory method, and then each of the bike parts is going to be returned as the correct type of bike parts. So let's just run this to make sure it still works. And I can see that for the first one, it uses all the right values for a road bike, and the second one, all the right values for a mountain bike. So this method is now much shorter and a bit more readable, but more importantly is actually much more flexible. So if I add a new bike type with all the different bike parts, I don't need to change the BikeManufacturer class at all. I don't need to change anything in this method, it's just really nicely decoupled. Instead, I would have to create a new factory for the new bike type and add that type to the Map in the FactoryMaker class. So in here, I would just add a new value to the Map inside this static block. So the client, which in this case is the bike manufacturer, doesn't need to know anything about how the different types of bike are created.

#### [Solution: The Abstract Factory pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-abstract-factory-pattern?u=76281980&t=5)** I'm going to talk through my solution to the abstract factory pattern exercise. So the first thing I did was I created an interface for creating user interface factories. So that's a class called UserInterfaceFactory. And in here, I created two methods; createButton and createScrollbar. And then I implemented this interface for each color. So first of all, I created a BlueUserInterfaceFactory. And in this one, I returned a blue button in the createButton method and a blue scroll bar in the createScrollbar method. Then I did the same for red. So I have a RedUserInterfaceFactory, and in this one, I'm returning a red button and a red scroll bar. Then I needed a factory to create the right factory. So I created a new class called FactoryMaker. And in this class, I created a new Map. And the key is a string, and the value is a supplier that returns a UserInterfaceFactory. And I call this UserInterfaceFactories and this is a HashMap. Then I had a static block to populate the Map. So first, I put the key "BLUE" and the value BlueUserInterfaceFactory::new. And then I put the key "RED" and the value RedUserInterfaceFactory::new. Then I have my static method called createFactory which passes in a color which is a string, and then it checks if that color is inside my Map. So it says "userInterfaceFactories.get" and passes in the color and checks that it's not null. So if that color is in the Map, it returns the value.

And if I pass in red, it returns a new RedUserInterfaceFactory. And if the color is not in the Map, it throws a new IllegalArgumentException that just says "Color not supported." So then all I needed to do was update the App class. So if I look in the App class, I've now updated this createUserInterface method, so I'm still passing in the color. But then I've got a new variable called userInterfaceFactory and I'm using my static createFactory method in the FactoryMaker class to create the right user interface factory. Then I'm using that to create the right type of button and the right type of scroll bar, and I'm using those to create a new user interface. So that's one way of implementing the abstract factory pattern. This is the most complex exercise so far. So very well done if you did complete it.

### 8. Structural Design Patterns

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What are structural design patterns?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-are-structural-design-patterns?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-are-structural-design-patterns?u=76281980&t=0)** Structural [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) are all to do with how classes and objects are put together to form larger structures. As these structures get bigger over time, they can often get more complex. So these patterns are ways of tackling that complexity and simplifying these structures. There are two different categories of structural design pattern. There are ones that focus on classes and ones that focus on objects. You can think of the difference between class patterns and object patterns as the difference between "is a" relationships and "has a" relationships. For example, a car is a vehicle and a car has an engine. Class patterns are concerned with the "is a" relationships. They're all to do with how classes are structured and interacts, and there's a particular focus on how inheritance is used to create structures. Several of these patterns make use of interfaces to define shared functionality. Structural object patterns, on the other hand, are concerned with the "has a" relationships. They're all about how to use objects, and structural patterns can allow you to change the behavior of individual objects at runtime.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)


### 9. The Adapter Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Adapter pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-adapter-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The adapter pattern allows classes to work together when they normally wouldn't be able to. Imagine a plug adapter. If you have a US plug and you travel to Europe, you can't put your plug straight in the socket. You need an adapter, and this allows the US plug and the European plug socket to work together. This is the concept that the adapter pattern is all about. You have two classes that can't normally work together because they have incompatible interfaces, and then you put an adapter in between them so that they can work together. This is a pattern that's most often used when working with existing code, rather than writing new code. If you are writing something new, you just make sure that the classes you wanted to communicate would be able to. But if you're working with existing code and you can't or don't want to change it, the adaptive pattern can be a nice solution. One example is where you have a legacy code base and you need to add some new features to it. You might not want to change the existing code base because you don't want to risk breaking something, especially if there are no tests, or you might not even have access to it if it's in a third-party library. There are two different types of adaptive pattern; the class version and the object version. The object version is more common in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) because the class version relies on multiple inheritance. This isn't technically possible in Java, because you can't have classes that extend more than one other class, but a version of this can be achieved by using interfaces. The object's version of the pattern uses composition. This means that the adapter has an instance of the adaptee as a field for that class. So that's an example of a "has a" relationship.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=93)** The class version uses inheritance, so in this case the adapter is a subclass of the adaptee. So that's an "is a" relationship. These two patterns can both be useful depending on the specific use case. So in this chapter, I'll show you both ways of doing it. And I'll talk about the drawbacks and the benefits of each one.

>
