#### [Implement the object Adapter pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-object-adapter-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-object-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Here, I have an app for submitting online job applications. When an application is submitted, it has to have a document attached. The problem is that some of the classes are incompatible and don't work together. So first of all, I have a class called OnlineApplication. And this class has a field called DocumentUploader. And then in the constructor, a documentUploader object is passed in and the field is set to that object. Then there's a method called uploadDocument. And inside here, I'm saying "documentUploader.upload." So let's have a look at this DocumentUploader. This is actually just an interface, and this defines this upload method. And there's one class that implements this and that's the PdfDocumentUploader class. So this overrides the upload method. And inside here, it prints out two messages. One saying "Preparing PDF document," and then another saying "Uploading PDF document." And then there's an App class. So this has a main method in it. And inside here, I'm creating a new PdfDocumentUploader. Then I'm creating a new OnlineApplication and passing in the PdfDocumentUploader. And then I'm saying "onlineApplication.uploadDocument." So if I run the app, I can see in the console that it says "Preparing PDF document" and then "Uploading PDF document." So there's one more class that I haven't talked about yet, and that's the WordDocumentUploader class. So this class does something very similar to the PDF version, but it uploads [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) documents instead of PDFs. And the problem is that I can't use it with the OnlineApplication class because it doesn't implement document uploader,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-object-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=96)** and that class expects a document uploader in its constructor. And I've decided I'm not going to touch any of the code in this WordDocumentUploader class. I'm just going to leave it as it is. So I'm going to solve this by creating an adapter. So I'm going to create a new [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) class, and I'm going to call it "WordDocumentAdapter." And this new class is going to implement the DocumentUploader interface. So first of all, this class is going to have a field of type "WordDocumentUploader." So I'm going to say "private final WordDocumentUploader wordDocumentUploader." And then I'm going to have a constructor. So I'm going to say "public class WordDocumentAdapter." And then inside here, I'm going to initialize the uploader. So I'm going to say "wordDocumentUploader equals new WordDocumentUploader." And I just need to get rid of class from the constructor. And then finally, to make this class compile, I need to override the upload method. So at the bottom, I'm going to add @Override. And then I'm going to say "public void upload." And then inside this method, I'm going to say "wordDocumentUploader.uploadWordDocument." So this means that I can use this as a document uploader and call the upload method. And then that in turn will call the uploadWordDocument method, which is not compatible with the OnlineApplication class. So now let's give it a go and see if it works.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-object-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=191)** So I'm going to go back to the App class. And inside this main method, I'm first of all going to say "var wordDocumentUploader equals new WordDocumentAdapter. And then I'm going to say "var onlineApplication2 equals new OnlineApplication." And into the constructor, I'm going to pass in my wordDocumentUploader. And then I'm going to say "onlineApplication2.uploadDocument." So if I run this now, it should print out that it's preparing and uploading a PDF. And then also that is preparing and uploading a Word document. So I'm going to run this. And I can see that it says "Preparing PDF document," "Uploading PDF document," and then "Preparing Word document" and "Uploading Word document." So that's how you can use the adapter pattern to allow classes to work together when they otherwise wouldn't be able to.

#### [Implement the class Adapter pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-class-adapter-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-class-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** My app for uploading online job applications now uses the adapter pattern so that it can upload [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) documents as well as PDF documents. But there's one small drawback. So if I run the app, I can see in the output that there's a very small difference between the messages it prints out. So it says "Preparing PDF document" and then three dots. But when it says "Preparing Word document," there's no three dots at the end. And I can't actually do anything about this because in the adapter, it just calls the method on the wordDocumentUploader objects. So if I want to be able to override the behavior of the adaptive class, it might be better to use the class AdapterPattern. So let's have a look at the Adapter class. So this is WordDocumentAdapter. And at the moment, it just calls wordDocumentUploader.uploadWordDocument, and there's nothing I can do to change the behavior inside this method, because the point of this pattern is that I'm not touching the code inside wordDocumentUploader. So the first thing I'm going to do to be able to override this method is I'm going to make this WordDocumentAdapter class extend the WordDocumentUploader class. So I've added extends WordDocumentUploader. And I'm still keeping implements DocumentUploader because I still need this upload method to be overridden. And the next thing I'm going to do is I'm going to remove this constructor and the private final field. And now I can actually just call uploadWordDocument directly. And that's because it now extends WordDocumentUploader where this method is defined.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-class-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=92)** So this is a bit nicer already because there's less code and there's one less objects that I need to create. And the other nice thing is that if I want to change the uploadWordDocument method, I can actually override it now in this class. So I'm going to go to the bottom of the class and I'm going to add another @Override. And then I'm going to say "public void uploadWordDocument," and now I can put whatever I want inside that method. So I'm going to say "System.out.println," and then inside here, I'm going to say "Preparing Word document." And then I can put the three dots at the end. And then I also still want the message that says "Uploading Word document." So I'm going to have another System.out.println and pass in that second message as well. So now if I go back to the App class and I run this again, in the console output this time, I can see it says "Preparing PDF document" and three dots and then to match, it says "Preparing Word document" and the same three dots. So that's the class version of the adapter pattern. One drawback compared to the object version is that with the object version, a single adapter can work with multiple adaptees. So, for example, with this WordDocumentAdapter, I can only use it now with WordDocumentUploaders. So, for example, if I had other file types, I wouldn't be able to use this adapter with them. I'd need different adapters for each one. So those are the differences between the two implementations, and which one you use depends on which of the drawbacks matters less to you for your use case.

#### [Solution: The Adapter pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-adapter-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=5)** I'm going to walk through my solution to this adapter pattern challenge. So the first thing I'm going to do is create my adapter. So I'm going to create a new class, and I'm going to call it "FrenchMessageAdapter." And this class is going to implement the LocalizedMessage interface. So at the end I'm going to add implements LocalizedMessage. And this class is going to have a field of type frenchLocalizedMessage. So I'm going to say "private final FrenchLocalizedMessage frenchLocalizedMessage." And I'm going to set that inside a constructor. So then I'm going to say "public FrenchMessageAdapter" and inside the constructor, I'm going to say "frenchLocalizedMessage equals new FrenchLocalizedMessage." Now, I need to override the sayHello method. So at the bottom, I'm going to add an @Override, and then I'm going to say "public void sayHello." And inside this method, I'm going to say "frenchLocalizedMessage.sayBonjour." So now let's see if it works. So I'm going to go back to the App class. And inside this main method, I'm going to create a new French localized message using my adapter. So I'm going to say "var frenchLocalizedMessage equals new FrenchMessageAdapter." Then I'm going to say "var frenchGreeting equals new Greeting." And I'm going to pass in my frenchLocalizedMessage.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/solution-the-adapter-pattern?u=76281980&t=101)** Then I can say "frenchGreeting.print," and let's run this. And now I can see in the output that it says "Hello" and then Bonjour. So that's my solution to the adapter pattern. If you managed to do it this way then congratulations. And also, if you managed to do it using the class version, congratulations as well. And particularly, well done if you managed to try out both different versions.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Identifiers:** frenchlocalizedmessage (6), sayhello (2), frenchgreeting (2), saybonjour (1)

### 10. The Bridge Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Bridge pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-bridge-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-bridge-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The bridge pattern is all about using inheritance while maintaining flexibility. When we inherit from other classes, those classes become quite closely coupled together. But what if we want the child classes to have more flexibility about how they're maintained? Let's say there's a hierarchy of shapes. So at the top is the shape interface. And below that, there are the classes that extend shape like circle and square and so on. But then say the different types of shape can also be different colors. So there's a separate hierarchy with an interface called color and different implementations called red, blue, etc. So how do we handle this? One option would be to add new classes for all the different shapes and all the different color combinations, but this would quickly lead to the need for lots of new classes. The solution is to use the bridge pattern. In this pattern, the shape interface has a color field, and this can be set for all the different objects. So that means we don't have to make all those extra classes. Instead, when a new concrete shape is created, the color is set as a field. So the main idea for the bridge pattern is to use composition over inheritance. In this case, a shape has a color rather than a shape is a color. So you'd use this pattern when you have a class hierarchy, but you want to keep it flexible and allow the different concrete implementations to be configured in different ways.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### [Implement the Bridge pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-bridge-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-bridge-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** In this example, I have an app for a store that creates custom clothing. So there's an interface called ClothingItem and this has a single method called getType. And then there are two classes that implements this interface which are Hoodie and Shirt. So if I look in the Hoodie class, this overrides the getType method and prints out the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "Hoodie." And in the Shirt class, it overrides the getType method and prints out the word "Shirt." Then I have a class called App which has a main method in it, and inside there, I'm creating a new Hoodie object and saying "hoodie.getType" and then a new Shirt object and saying "shirt.getType." So if I run this, it prints out the word "Hoodie" and then the word "Shirt." However, I also want the ClothingItem to be available in multiple sizes. So I also have an interface called Size. And this has a single method called get. And then there are two implementations of the Size interface, which are AdultSize and ChildSize. So in the AdultSize class in the get method, it prints out the word "Adult." And in the ChildSize class in the get method, it prints out the word "Child." So I want to be able to have adult size hoodies, child size hoodies, adult size shirts, and child size shirts. But what's the best way to do this? I could refactor this so I have four different concrete implementations of ClothingItem, but that would mean that every time I create a new ClothingItem, I would have to create two new classes for it, one for each size. And if I add more sizes later on, I'd have to create a new class for each different clothing type. So instead, I'm going to use the bridge pattern.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-bridge-pattern?u=76281980&t=95)** So I'm going to go to the ClothingItem interface. And in here, I'm going to create a new method called getSize. And it's going to return a size type. So I'm going to say "Size" and then getSize. So now I need to update the concrete classes. So I'm going to start with the Hoodie class. So let's go to that one. And the first thing I'm going to do is I'm going to create a field for the size. So inside here, I'm going to say "private final Size size," and the size is going to be set by passing a size into the constructor. So this class doesn't have a constructor yet, so I'm going to create one. And I'm going to say "public Hoodie" and then pass in a Size object. And then inside this constructor, I can say "this.size equals size." And now I need to override the getSize method. So at the bottom of the class, I'm going to add an @Override, and then I'm going to say "public Size getSize." And then in here, I'm just going to return the size field. So I'm going to say "return size." And now I just need to do the same for the Shirt class. So I'm going to go to shirts, and again I'm going to add a field called size. So I'm going to say "private final Size size," and then again I need a constructor. So I'm going to say "public Shirt" and then pass in a size. And inside the constructor, I'm going to say "this.size equals size." And then again, I need to override the getSize method. And in fact, I think I can copy that from the Hoodie class.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-bridge-pattern?u=76281980&t=191)** So I'm going to go back to Hoodie, copy everything in the getSize method, go back to Shirt again, and paste that in here. So finally, I'm going to go back to the App class, and now I need to pass a size into the constructor for the clothing items. So let's say I want a child size hoodie. So inside the Hoodie constructor, I'm going to say "new ChildSize." And let's make the shirt an adult size. So inside the constructor for this one, I'm going to say "new AdultSize." And just to check that it's worked, I'll print out the size of each. So under hoodie.getType, I'm going to say "hoodie.getSize.get." And then at the bottom of the method, I'm going to say "shirt.getSize.get." And now if I run this again, I can see that it says "Hoodie" and "Child" and then "Shirt" and "Adult." So this is how the bridge pattern can be used to avoid ending up with a complex hierarchy. Instead, I've used composition to pass in the configuration to the different classes.

### 11. The Composite Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Composite pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-decorator-pattern-23224165?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-decorator-pattern-23224165?u=76281980&t=0)** The composite pattern allows you to trace objects and groups of objects in the same way. Let's see how this looks with an example. Say you have a checklist app that contains a checklist made up of to-do items. So there'd be a class called Checklist, and that class would contain a collection of ToDoItem objects. And let's say I want to have a method that will tell me if a to-do item has been completed or not. And I also want to have the same method in the Checklist class. The checklist is complete if all of the to-do items in the checklist are also complete. The way I do this is with the composite pattern, so you'd have either an interface or an abstract class that both the composite and the parts of the composite both inherit from. So in the interface, I could have a method called isComplete. The ToDo class can implement this. And then in the Checklist class, it can also implement this same method. And for each to-do item in the list, it can call this isComplete method for each of those items. So the composite pattern lets you treat objects and composites of objects in the same way. And it also means that the clients using the composites can ignore differences between the composite and its parts. For example, a client could use the isComplete method for the checklist and also for the individual to-do items in the same way, because they inherit from the same interface.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Identifiers:** iscomplete (3)
> **Definitions:** means that (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)

#### [Implement the Composite pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator?u=76281980&t=0)** The app I have here is an implementation of a checklist, and I'm going to be using the composite pattern to make it easy to handle groups of to-do items in the checklist. So the first class I have here is the TodoItem class. This has a string field called description which is set in the constructor. And then there's also a field called completed, which is set to false when a new to-do item is created. And then there's a method called complete which sets the completed field to true. Next, there's a class called Checklist. So this Checklist has an array list of items called todoItems. So it's initialized with an empty array list. And then there's a method called addTodoItem which adds a to-do item to the list. Finally, there's a class called App. So this class has a main method inside it where I'm creating a new checklist, and then I'm adding three to-do items to the list. And then I'm calling the complete method on the first to-do item. So the checklist has one completed item and two uncompleted items. A checklist in this case is a composite because it contains a collection of other similar items. And now I want to be able to see if an item is complete. But I also want to see if a checklist is complete. So I want the same functionality to check if to-do items are complete, and if a checklist is complete, which is essentially a list of to-do items. So I'm going to create an interface that both classes are going to extend. So I'm going to create a new class and I'm going to call it "Completable." And this new class is going to be an interface. I'm going to give it a single method,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator?u=76281980&t=94)** which is going to be a void method called isCompleted. So first, I'm going to make this TodoItem class implement this interface. So I'm going to go to the TodoItem, and at the top, I'm going to add implements Completable. So I need to override the isCompleted method. So at the bottom of the class, I'm going to add a new method. And I'm going to say "@Override" and I'm going to say "public void isCompleted." In this method, I'm just going to print out a message saying what the to-do item is and if it's completed or not. So I'm going to say "System.out.println," and in here, I'm going to say "Todo item." And then I want to print out the description inside quote marks. So to escape the quote mark, I'm going to have a backslash and then a quote and then I'm going to say "+ description." And then again, I'm going to have an escaped quote mark and I'm going to say "is completed." And then I want to print out the completed field. So I'm just going to say "+ completed." Then I'm going to do the same for the Checklist class. So I'm going to go to Checklist, and again, I'm going to say "implements completable." And at the bottom, I'm going to add the isCompleted method. So I'm going to say "@Override" and then public void isCompleted. And in this isCompleted method, I'm going to loop through each of the to-do items in the list and call the isCompleted
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator?u=76281980&t=187)** method on each one. So I'm going to say "todoItems.forEach," and then I'm actually going to use a method reference. So I'm going to say "TodoItem" and then two colons and then I'm going to say "isCompleted." So that's going to loop through the array list and call the isCompleted method for each to-do item. So now if I go back to the App class, I'm going to test this out. So in the main method at the bottom, I'm going to say "checklist.isCompleted." So this isCompleted method is going to call the isCompleted method on all of the items in the checklist. So if I run this, I can see it prints out all of the to-do items, and the first one is completed and the last two are incomplete. So that's how the composite pattern lets you treat groups or composites of objects in a uniform way.

### 12. The Decorator Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Decorator pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-decorator-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-decorator-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The decorator pattern change the behavior of an object without having to create a new subclass for that behavior. So say, for example, that you have an app for creating rooms that are decorated in different styles. So you might start off with a blank EmptyRoom class, but then you want to have a room with a couch in it. So you'd have a room with Couch class. And then you might have a room with a couch and a table in it. So you'd have a class representing a room with a couch and a table. And then there might be lots of different combinations for lots of different types of furniture. You can see that if I had different subclasses for all these different possible combinations, it would get out of hand very quickly. This slide only shows a few different possible combinations of decorated rooms, and there's already a lot on there. So the solution is to use a decorator pattern. The first part of this pattern is that you have an interface at the top. So in this case, a Room interface, and then a concrete implementation of that Room interface. In this case, this room implementation represents an empty room with no decoration. Next, there's another interface or an abstract class underneath the Room interface called RoomDecorator. Then there were concrete implementations of this decorator class for each of the different decorations you want to have. These decorations take a concrete room object as an argument to their constructor. So, for example, you create a room object and then a room with a sofa decorator and pass in the room objects. And you can do this as many times as you want. For example, you can create a room with a lamp and then pass in a room with a sofa objects. So then you'd end up with a room with a sofa and a lamp. So this design cuts down on the number of classes we need, because you don't need one
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-decorator-pattern?u=76281980&t=92)** for each different combination of decorations. Each new object that's created can have its own specific functionality without affecting other objects.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Analogies:** for example (3)

#### [Change behavior dynamically with a decorator](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator-23218147?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator-23218147?u=76281980&t=0)** In this example, I have a simplified version of an app for logging in users to an application, and I'm going to use the decorator pattern to be able to create users with different privileges. So at the moment, I have an interface called User which defines a method called printPrivileges. And then there's a class called BasicUser which implements user. And inside the printPrivileges method, it prints out a message saying "Can log in." So that means that a basic user has the privilege to log in to the app. So I want to be able to have users with different combinations of privileges. So I'm going to create a Decorator class to handle that. I'm going to create a new [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) class and I'm going to call it UserDecorator. And this is going to be an abstract class. So I'm going to add the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "abstract" before class. And then I'm going to add at the end, implements User. So it implements the user interface as well. This class is going to have a field of type user, which is the user that's going to be decorated. So I'm going to add a fields and say "private final User user." And then I'm going to have a constructor to set the user that's passed in. So I'm going to say "public UserDecorator," and then pass in a user objects. And inside the constructor, I'm going to say "this.user equals user." And I still need to override the printPrivileges method. So at the bottom of this class, I'm going to add an @Override, And then I'm going to say "public void printPrivileges." And inside this method, I'm going to say "user.printsPrivileges."
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator-23218147?u=76281980&t=95)** So that's going to print out the privileges of the user that I pass in to the constructor. Now, I need to create concrete implementations of this abstract class. So let's say I want to give a user admin privileges. So I'm going to create another new Java class and I'm going to call this one AdminUser. And this class is going to extend the UserDecorator class. So I'm adding extends UserDecorator. So first of all, I'm going to create a constructor in this class that passes in a user object. So I'm going to say "public AdminUser" and pass in a user objects. And then inside this constructor, I'm just going to say "super" and then pass in that user. So that's just going to set the user field from the UserDecorator to the user that's passed into this constructor. Next, I need to override the printPrivileges method again. So I'm going to say "@Override" and then public void printPrivileges. And first inside this method, I'm going to say "super.printPrivileges." So that's going to print out the privileges of the user that I passed into the constructor. Then I'm going to add my own custom privileges here. So let's say that because they're an admin user, they can also create new users. So I'm going to add System.out.println and I'm going to print out a message that says, "Can create new users." So now I can update the Login class to test it works. So I'm going to go to Login, and in here, I'm creating a new basic user and then saying "user.printPrivileges."
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/change-behavior-dynamically-with-a-decorator-23218147?u=76281980&t=190)** So if I run this at the moment, I can see that at the moment the user can log in. So I'm going to add a new line. And first of all, I'm just going to print out an empty line to make it a bit easier to read. And then I'm going to say "var adminUser equals new AdminUser." And I'm going to pass in the basic user. Then I'm going to say "adminUser.printPrivileges." And if I run this again, I can see that this time it says "Can log in." And then for the second user, it says "Can log in" and then "Can create new users." So that means I've added admin privileges to the user. So that's how you can use the decorator pattern to add functionality dynamically to objects.

### 13. The Facade Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Facade pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-facade-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-facade-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) facade can mean a deceptive outward appearance, and in this case, it means that you have a simple interface that hides the complexity of the code behind it. For example, think about when you write an email and click on "Send." There's a lot going on behind the scenes when we click on that send button, but most people aren't thinking about the technical things behind the scenes every time they send an email. And we certainly don't need to know how email protocols work in order to send one. So in this example, the send button is a facade that hides the complexity of sending an email. Facades are needed in software because it's common to break large systems down into smaller parts, often with lots of dependencies between them. This is generally a good thing because it makes systems more reusable and easier to customize, but it can also make it harder to use. A client that wants to use a system like this would have to interact with all the classes underneath. This means that it can make the most of the possible customizations, but sometimes the client might not care about those and it still has to use all the classes anyway. So a facade is used as a single, simpler interface to these complex systems. Then the client can just use this simple facade interface to do everything at once. As well as being simpler, it helps to minimize the dependencies between the client and the subsystems. The other nice thing is that you don't have to use the facade if you don't want to just because it's there. You can directly access the classes or subsystems underneath if you want to. So to summarize, the facade pattern can be helpful when you want to provide a simple interface to a complex subsystem.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) (1)
> **Definitions:** means that (2), is a  (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **UI Navigation:** click on (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)

#### [Implement the Facade pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-facade-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-facade-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** I have an application here that's used for encrypting strings. Encryption is quite a complicated process in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) with quite a few steps in it, and I'm using lots of different classes from the JDK here to do this. So, for example, I'm using key generator, secret key, secure random, IV parameter spec, and so on. So don't worry if you don't understand what this code is doing, and I'm not going to talk it through and explain it, but the point is that it's complicated and uses lots of different classes. So basically, what this code does is it takes a string, so I've given it an input string of some text to encrypt, and then at the bottom, it prints out the string that it's encrypted. So it says "Encrypted String" and then the result. So if I run the app, I can see it prints out encrypted string and then basically a series of random characters. So this is useful functionality, and I might want to use it in lots of different places in my app, but it would be a bit of a pain having to go through all this setup every time I want to use it, especially as I don't care about how the encryption is done. So I'm going to create a facade. So I'm going to create a new Java class and I'm going to call it EncryptionFacade. And inside this class, I'm going to create a new method called encrypt. So I'm going to say "public void encrypt." And it's going to take a string called inputs as an argument. And now I'm going to copy and paste all the code from the clients that does the encryption into this method. So I'm going to go back to the Client class and I'm just going to copy everything inside this main method
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-facade-pattern?u=76281980&t=95)** and paste it inside the EncryptionFacade, encrypt method. And I'm actually going to get rid of that string input, because I'm going to be passing that in as an argument to the method. And I also need to copy across the exceptions. So I'm going to go back to Client and copy all of these exceptions from the method signature and I'm going to paste them next to this encrypt method. So now I'm going to go back to the Client class again, and I'm going to remove all of this code inside the main method except for the input string. And I'm going to create a variable for my EncryptionFacade. So I'm going to say "var encryptionFacade equals new EncryptionFacade." Then I can say "encryptionFacade.encrypt" and then pass in the input string. So if I run this again, I can see that it still works the same as before. It encrypts the string and prints out this string of random characters. So in this case, I've created a facade to hide code that uses lots of classes from the JDK, but it's also a common use case to use a facade that calls your own classes as well.

### 14. The Flyweight Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Flyweight pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-flyweight-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-flyweight-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The flyweight pattern is all about minimizing memory usage. It does this by reusing objects that have already been created rather than using new ones. One example of where this pattern might be used is in a [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) processor app. When someone types a sentence, each letter could be a new object. So if someone writes a long documents, there are going to be lots of objects. But with a flyweight pattern, you have only one object for each letter, so one a object, one b object, and so on, and these can be reused. This way, less memory is used for storing all the different letters as typed. The way this is usually implemented in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) is that you have a factory class with a map, for example a HashMap. Then when someone types the letter a, it checks if there's an object with the key "a" inside the map. If not, then it creates one and puts it in the map. If there's one already there, it reuses that one. The only thing that's different about each letter a, for example, is its position in the document. So the object has states, which is immutable, for example the name of the letter. This immutable state should only be controlled through the factory. And then there should be mutable states, for example the position. This is a similar concept to a cache. The idea is that if the data is already there and can be reused, it should be. This is especially useful if it's computationally expensive to create new objects, so you should use this pattern when you want to save memory by reusing objects instead of creating new ones.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) (1), [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (4)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### [Implement the Flyweight pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-fyweight-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-fyweight-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** This is an app that's used for placing orders in a restaurant. So first of all, there's a class called Order which has a string field called name. And then I'm passing the name into the constructor. And inside the constructor, I'm saying "this.name equals name." Also, inside the constructor, there's a call to thread.sleep for three seconds. So this means that it waits three seconds each time while the order is being prepared. So each time it creates a new order object, it takes at least three seconds to actually create it. So if someone places a lot of orders, that's going to slow my app down quite a bit. And I also have a System.out.println before and after the three seconds, just so I can see when the object was first starting to be created and when it's finished being created. Then there's a class called the Restaurant class, which has a main method in it. Inside the main method, I'm creating three new order objects and they're all pizza orders. And then later, I'm creating three new burger order objects. Underneath the orders, I'm also printing out the objects reference for each one. And that's just to show that a new object is being created for each new order. So if I run the app, I can see that it's taking a while to run because it's waiting three seconds each time it creates a new objects. So it's finished creating the Pizza objects, and now it's moved on to the Burger objects. Another is finished running, I can see that the object's reference is different for each one. So that's just to show that it's creating six different separate objects here. But actually, I don't really need six new objects. I could have one Pizza object that I can reuse, and one Burger object that I could reuse. So I'm going to implement the flyweight pattern to help with this. So first of all, I'm going to create a new class,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-fyweight-pattern?u=76281980&t=95)** and I'm going to call it OrderFactory. And inside this class, I'm going to create a new map. So I'm going to say "private" and is going to be a static map because it belongs to the class. Then I'm going to say "final Map." And the key is going to be a string, which is the name of the order, and then the value is going to be the order object itself. And I'm going to call this orderCache. Then I'm going to say "equals new HashMap." So next, I need a method to create the order. So I'm going to say "public" and it's going to be a static method. So I'm going to say "static Order," and then I'm going to call it "createOrder." And this method is going to take the name of the order as an argument. So I'm going to say "String name" inside the brackets. So the first thing I need to do inside this method is I need to check if an order objects with the same name already exists in the map. So first of all, I'm going to say "if orderCache.containsKey" and pass a name. Then if the name is already in the map, I can return the object associated with that name. So then I can say "return orderCache.get" and pass in the name. Then if it hasn't found an order, I'm going to create a new one. So I'm going to say "var order equals new Order," and pass in the name to the constructor. Then I'm going to save the new Order object in the map. So I'm going to say "orderCache.put"
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-fyweight-pattern?u=76281980&t=190)** and pass in the name and then the new Order object that I just created as the value. Then I returned that new order. So at the end I can say "return order." So if I create a Pizza object for the first time, for example, it will create the new order with pizza as the name, then it will put it inside the map and then it will return that new pizza order. And then the second time it tries to create a pizza, it will look in the map, see that there's already one with the name pizza, and it will return that to existing objects. So the nice thing is, I can actually simplify this quite a bit by using a handy method on the Map class called computeIfAbsent. And this basically does all of the logic I have inside this method for me. So what I can do is I can actually replace all of this. So delete all of that and I can say "return orderCache.computeIfAbsent" and then the first argument is going to be the name. And then the second argument is a function. So I want to create a new order in this case. So I can use a method reference and say "Order::new." And that basically does the same as all of the logic that I just wrote out and then deleted. So it will check if the name is in the map, and if it is, then return the value associated with it. And if it's not, then it creates a new object, saves it in the map with that name, and returns the new object's. Some people might prefer the way I had it before, as it's a bit more obvious what the code is doing, especially if you don't know how this computeIfAbsent method works. So which one you use is down to you and your personal preference.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-fyweight-pattern?u=76281980&t=284)** So now I can go and update the Restaurant class to use my new OrderFactory. So I'm going to go back to restaurants and instead of saying new Order and passing in pizza, I can use my new static method. So I'm going to replace that with OrderFactory.createOrder and pass in pizza. And then I can copy this for pizza2 and pizza3. So each time I'm going to replace the call to new Order and instead use my OrderFactory.createOrder. Then I can do the same for the burgers. But I'm going to pass in the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) "burger" instead of pizza. So I'm going to copy and paste this for burger2 and for burger3 as well. So now if I run the app again, it's a bit quicker to run this time because it only has to wait three seconds once for the pizza and once for the burger object. And I just want to show you that the object's reference is the same for each one. So instead of creating six new objects, it's just creating one pizza object and reusing it and one burger object and reusing it.

### 15. The Proxy Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Proxy pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-proxy-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-proxy-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** In the proxy pattern, a proxy is a class that controls access to the functionality of another class. For example, imagine that you have a class that does some expensive setup when you create a new instance of it. When describing this pattern, this class is sometimes referred to as the subject. So let's say, for example, that the first time the subject is used, it has lots of expensive calls to a database, and then later it does something with the data. This code might be in a third-party library, so you might not have access to it. In this case, you only want the expensive setup to happen once, the first time the object is created. After that, you just want to do the part where it processes the data. You don't really want the classes that call this code to have to implement the logic about only doing this setup once. So in this case, you'd have a proxy object in between, and that controls access to the class with the expensive setup. Then the client just calls the proxy. It doesn't need to know about this complex setup. So the proxy can be used to only initialize objects on demand for optimization. Or for example, if you only want people with certain privileges to be able to do something, the proxy could be used to control who performs a certain action. Another use case is when you have an object that resides in a different address space. You could use a proxy to hide that fact from the client. So essentially, a proxy controls access to another objects, which you might want to do for a number of different reasons.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Prerequisites:** setup (5)
> **Analogies:** for example (3), imagine (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

### 16. Behavioral Design Patterns

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What are behavioral design patterns?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-are-behavioral-design-patterns?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/what-are-behavioral-design-patterns?u=76281980&t=0)** Behavioral [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) are about how objects assign responsibilities between each other. They're concerned with how objects are connected, how they communicate with each other, and how responsibilities are assigned between them. And they're often to do with complex control flows. Like with structural design patterns, there are some behavioral patterns that are focused on classes and some that are focused on objects. Class behavioral patterns use inheritance to share behavior between different classes. But most of the behavioral patterns I'll be showing you are object patterns. Some of these look at how objects can work together to do things that couldn't be done by a single object on its own. Some other object patterns are more focused on encapsulating behavior in a single object, and then delegating requests to it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Design Patterns](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Design%20Patterns.md) (2)


### 17. The Chain of Responsibility Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Chain of Responsibility pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-chain-of-responsibility-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-chain-of-responsibility-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** The aim of the chain of responsibility pattern is to decouple the centre of a request from the receiver of the request. Say, for example, you have a logger. Sometimes you want the logger to print output to the console, and sometimes you want it to write to a file. So when you have some code that logs something, at that point, it needs to know whether to write the log to the console or to a file. The chain of responsibility pattern can be used as a way of working out which one to send it to at runtime. For example, it could first check if the logger is a type of logger that prints to the console. If it is, then it prints the log to the console and that's the end of the chain. However, if the answer is no, it could pass it along the chain and check if it's a logger that prints to a file. If it is, then it prints the log to the file and that's the end of the chain. In this scenario, it's the responsibility of the logger that prints to the console to pass it along the chain to the next possible option. One thing to be careful of is making sure you don't accidentally end up stuck in a circle. So, for example, if the file logger has a reference to the console logger as the next one to check, you would end up in an endless loop. If you have lots of links in the chain, this could accidentally happen quite easily. The advantage of using this pattern is that it decouples the center of the request from the receiver. For example, the logger doesn't need to know in advance of its writing to the console or a file. One thing to consider is that there's no guarantee that the request is going to be handled. If it reaches the end of the chain and hasn't found an option that fits, then nothing will happen with the request.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Analogies:** for example (4)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Warnings:** be careful (1)

#### [Recognize where to use the Chain of Responsibility pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-the-chain-of-responsibility-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-the-chain-of-responsibility-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** In this app, I have a logger that can either print to the console or to a file. When you want to log something in the app, you send a logger request which specifies the message you want to log and the type of logger you want to use. At the moment, the type of logger in the request is coupled to the object that receives the request. So the first class to look at is the LoggerRequest. This class has two fields. It has a string field called message and then a field called loggerType. And both of these fields are set in the constructor. And then there are getter methods for both of these fields. And the loggerType is actually an enum at the bottom of the class, so a loggerType can either be console or file. Next there is the logger itself. So this is an abstract class which has a single method called log. And that takes a LoggerRequest as an argument. Then there are two implementations of this logger class. So the first one is the ConsoleLogger. This extends logger and overrides the log method, and in this one it prints out the requests message to the console. Then there's the FileLogger class. So this also extends logger and also overrides the log method. And in this one it's slightly more complicated. It writes the log message to a log file called log.txt. So this just writes the message to the file plus a new line and then closes the right connection. Then to show how it works, I've got a class called App which has a main method. And in here I'm creating a message called "Hello World", which is the thing that I want to log. Then I'm creating a logger request. So I'm passing in the message and then the logger type, which at the moment I've got as console.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/recognise-where-to-use-the-chain-of-responsibility-pattern?u=76281980&t=93)** Then I'm creating a new logger object. So I'm saying logger = new ConsoleLogger(); and then I'm setting logger.log and passing in the request. So if I run the app, I can see that at the moment, it prints out my message Hello World to the console because it's using the ConsoleLogger. So now let's say I want to change this to print it to the log.txt file instead. So in the request, I need to change the type from console to file. And then I also need to change the logger from a console logger to a file logger. So the fact that I'm having to update the logger, which is the thing that receives the request, is a sign that these two things are tightly coupled. So if I run the app again, this time it doesn't print anything to the console, but if I look inside the log.txt file, I can see it's put my message inside that file instead. So the functionality does work as I expect it to, but I want to decouple the logging request from the concrete logger type. And one good way to do that is to use the chain of responsibility pattern.

#### [Implement a successor chain](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-a-successor-chain?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-a-successor-chain?u=76281980&t=0)** This is a logging app that would benefit from using the chain of responsibility pattern to decouple the sender of the logging request from the concrete logger that's going to handle it. I'm going to create a chain of loggers to handle this scenario. The first thing to change is in the logger class. So I'm going to go to logger. And I'm going to make sure it has a reference to the next logger in the chain. So I'm going to create a new field and say public final logger and I'll call it nextLogger. And then inside this constructor I'm going to say super and pass in nextLogger. So that's just going to call the constructor from the logger class. I also need to update the log method. Now I need to update the app class, so I'm going to go
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-a-successor-chain?u=76281980&t=191)** back to app. And I'm going to introduce a new method to build the chain in the order that I want it. I'm going to start at the end of the chain and work my way backwards. So I want the file logger to be the last one that I check. So first I'm going to say var fileLogger = new FileLogger. And this is the end of the chain. So I'm just going to pass a null for the next logger. Then I'm going to create a ConsoleLogger. So I'm going to say var consoleLogger = new ConsoleLogger. And this time I'm going to pass in the file logger as the next logger to check. Then I'm going to return ConsoleLogger. And in my main method, where I'm creating the logger variable, I'm going to get rid of where it says new ConsoleLogger and instead I'm going to call my buildChain method. So that means that I no longer have to hardcode the type of the logger, which means it's now nicely decoupled from the request. And if I run the app, at the moment it prints to the console because I'm sending a console request. But if I change this to file and rerun it, this time it doesn't print to the console, but it does print to the log.txt file. So this is how the chain of responsibility pattern can be used to decouple the sender and receiver of a request,
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-a-successor-chain?u=76281980&t=286)** and adapts to the correct type dynamically.

### 18. The Command Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Command pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-command-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-command-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Sometimes when writing software, you need to issue a request, but you might not know in advance what's being requested or what's receiving the request. So let's think about a real-world example. Imagine someone has written an app for buying and selling things online. The app could be used by lots of different companies selling different things. One example might be a restaurant using it to allow customers to order food online. Another company might use it for selling clothes online. In both cases, the user journey is pretty much the same. The customer is browsing online and they decide they want to order something. So they input their order into the app and that information gets passed from the app to the company. But when the app is being written, we can't say explicitly what kind of thing the customer is going to order. It might be a pizza or it might be a pair of shoes. What exactly they're ordering is down to the different companies using the app. We also don't know what will happen after the order is sent, so there might be an immediate response saying the order is on its way or there might be some checks to see if it's in stock or there might be a reply saying that the order will arrive in a month. The command pattern is a way of making requests when you don't know anything about what exactly is being requested or what exactly is receiving it. It does this by encapsulating the request inside an object. This might seem a bit counterintuitive when you're used to object-oriented design. Normally, a command is something you would put in a method, not a class. But this is a useful way of decoupling the objects that invokes the command from the objects that know how to handle it and perform that command. So the command pattern is especially useful for commands that you might want to perform later on. For example, they're sometimes used to support undo and redo
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-command-pattern?u=76281980&t=94)** operations and things like queuing or logging requests to be performed at different times.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1), for example (1)

#### [Implement the Command pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-command-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-command-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** This is an app for adding items to a basket in an online store. So at the moment, there are only two classes; Item and OnlineStore. So this is the Item class and it has a field called id which is passed into the constructor. Then it has two methods; addToBasket and removeFromBasket. So in the addToBasket method, it just prints out a message saying, "Item id added to basket," and removeFromBasket prints out a message saying, "Item id removed from basket." Then in the OnlineStore class, I have a main method, and inside the main method, I'm creating three items; item1, item2, and item3. And then I'm adding all three items to the basket. And then I'm removing item2 from the baskets. So if I run this, I can see it says item1, item2, and item3 were all added to the basket, and then item2 was removed from the basket. But let's say that I don't want my OnlineStore class to know anything about the actions. At the moment, the OnlineStore is tightly coupled to how items are added to or removed from the baskets. So instead of calling them methods directly, I'm going to create classes for these actions. So first, I'm going to create another new class called AddItemToBasketOperation.
>

Then inside this method, I'm just going to say itemOperation.execute. So that gives me another level of abstraction between the store and the item operations. So now I can use this executor in my OnlineStore class. So I'm going to go back to OnlineStore, and after I've created the items, I'm going to create a new variable called ItemOperationExecutor. So I'm going to say itemOperationExecutor.doOperation, and then I'm going to pass in a new AddItemToBasketOperation. And then pass in item1 to that constructor. Then I can do the same for item2. So I'm going to copy that line and paste it and instead

> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/implement-the-command-pattern?u=76281980&t=287)** pass in item2. Then I'm going to paste it again, and this time, I'm going to pass in item3. So now I can remove the lines where I'm saying item1.addToBasket and the same for item2 and item3. And then to remove an item, I'm going to say itemOperationExecutor.doOperation and then I'm going to pass in a new RemoveItemFromBasketOperation and I'm going to pass in item2. So if I run this again, I can see I get the same output as before, so items one, two, and three are added to the basket and then item2 is removed from the basket. So now I've used the command pattern to decouple the online store from the internal workings of adding and removing items from a basket.

#### [Use the Command pattern to delay operations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/use-the-command-pattern-to-delay-operations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/use-the-command-pattern-to-delay-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** In this app, I've used the command pattern to decouple the store from the way items are added or removed from a basket. So I now have separate command classes for those two things, and I have a class called ItemOperationExecutor, which is used to execute them. But let's say that I don't want to perform the commands immediately. I want to wait until the customer goes to the checkout and then update the items in the baskets. The command pattern actually makes this quite easy to do. So I'm going to go to the ItemOperationExecutor class and I'm going to add a list for storing the commands in as a new field. So at the top, I'm going to say list, and it's going to contain ItemOperation objects. So I'm going to call it itemOperations, then I'm going to say equals new ArrayList. Then in the doOperation method, instead of executing the operation, I'm just going to add it to the list. So I'm going to remove where it says "itemOperation.execute," and I'm going to replace that with itemOperations.add and pass in the itemOperation. And I'm also going to rename this method to make it a bit clearer that it's not doing the operation, it's saving it for later. So I'm going to right-click on it and go to Rename Symbol and I'm going to call it queueOperation, instead. Then I'm going to add a new method for calling all of the operations in the list. So I'm going to have a new public method and it's going to have a void return type and I'm going to call it checkout. Then inside this method, I'm going to iterate over all the operations in the list and execute each one.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/use-the-command-pattern-to-delay-operations?u=76281980&t=93)** So I'm going to say itemOperations.forEach, and then I'm going to use a method reference. So I'm going to say ItemOperation, two colons, and then execute. Then I'm going to clear the list. So I'm going to say itemOperations.clear. So now I'm going to go back to my OnlineStore class. And if I run that up again now, nothing happens because I'm only queuing the operations. I'm not actually executing them. So now I'm going to add another call at the end of this main method to the checkout method. So I'm going to say itemOperationExecutor.checkout. And if I run that up again, when I call the checkout method, it then executes all of the operations. So it adds items one, two, and three to the basket, and then it removes item2. So some extra functionality can be added to the Executor class. Sometimes commands are added to a list or a stack or some other type of collection so that there can be undone later if needed.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Identifiers:** itemoperations (4), itemoperation (2), dooperation (1), queueoperation (1), foreach (1)
> **UI Navigation:** go to (2), right-click (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Cross-References:** go back to (1)

#### [Challenge: The Command pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/challenge-the-command-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/challenge-the-command-pattern?u=76281980&t=5)** In this exercise, you'll be implementing the command pattern in an app for turning a light on and off. So at the moment there's a class called Light and there's a Boolean field called isOn, which is set to false when you first create a light object. Then there's a method called turnOnLight which sets isOn to true and turnOffLight, which sets isOn to false. And then there's a method called isOn, which prints out a sentence saying light is either on or off, depending on the value of the Boolean. Then there's a class called App, and this has a main method in it. So we're creating a new light object in here which is initially off. Then we're seeing if it's off, turning the light on, seeing if it's on or off again, turning the light off, and then again printing out if it's on or off. So if I run this, we can see that initially the light is off, then it gets turned on and then it gets turned off again. So you should implement the command pattern in this app so that you have a command for turning the lights on, and a command for turning the lights off. After you've implemented the pattern, you should run the app again and check that you get the same output as before. So you should use the command pattern to make sure the App class isn't coupled to the methods for turning the lights on or off. Instead, it should use command classes to do this. So good luck and I'll be walking through the solution in my next video.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Identifiers:** ison (4), turnonlight (1), turnofflight (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Cross-References:** next video (1)

### 19. The Interpreter Pattern

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Understand the Interpreter pattern](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-interpreter-pattern?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-interpreter-pattern?u=76281980&t=0)** Imagine a scenario where two people are trying to communicate, but they don't speak the same language. A translator or an interpreter can join them and translates everything they say to each other. The interpreter pattern is a way of translating one language into another. So if you think of [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) Translate, for example, it takes the input language and interpret it into a new language. There are many different and varied scenarios where the interpreter pattern might be used. It could be used to write custom regular expressions or even write your own compiler. Or you could use it to translate human languages or parse [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) or even create a simple calculator. The idea of the interpreter pattern in [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) is that if the same problem keeps coming up, then you can express that problem as a sentence and interpret it. The interpreter defines a grammar for the language and then uses an abstract syntax tree to interpret it. This is quite a complicated concept, so let's see what it actually looks like. The first component in the interpreter pattern is the context. If you are translating a sentence into another language, the context would be the sentence. So the context is often just a string. The next part is an AbstractExpression class. This is an interface or an abstract class that defines a method used for interpreting the context. Each time the interpret method is called, the context is changed in some way. There are two different possible implementations of the AbstractExpression; terminal and non-terminal. A non-terminal expression calls the interpret method, which alters the context, and then it passes the context on to another expression until it reaches the terminal expression. A terminal expression is the last time the interpret method gets called.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/understand-the-interpreter-pattern?u=76281980&t=92)** So, for example, if you were translating a language, the context could be "Hello world." The non-terminal operations translate each [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) into the correct word in the new language. And when it gets to some punctuation, like a period or exclamation mark, it returns that and stops. This structure makes it easy to extend and implement the grammar. However, the interpreter pattern works best when the grammar being interpreted is simple. Complex grammars can be difficult to maintain because you would need a new expression class for every new rule you introduce.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) (2), [Google](../../Glossary/Service/Google.md) (1), [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) (1), [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) (1)
> **Tools:** terminal (6)
> **Definitions:** is an  (2), is a  (1), is called (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2), imagine (1)
> **Env Vars:** sql (1)
> **Cross-References:** coming up (1)

#### [Creating an interpreter for a language](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/creating-an-interpreter-for-a-language?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/creating-an-interpreter-for-a-language?u=76281980&t=0)** One of the possible uses of the interpreter pattern is interpreting code itself. You might have come across features in your IDE that show you hints when there are things in your code that it thinks could be improved. So one example is the organized input feature, where your IDE will rearrange your import statements in alphabetical order, and it will remove any unused ones. In this example, I'm going to implement a new feature similar to those used by your IDE to alter variable names so that they meet [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md) naming conventions. And I'm going to use the interpreter pattern. So the first thing I need to do is create an interface to define and interpret method. So I'm going to create a new Java class and I'm going to call it Expression. I'm going to change class to interface. And I'm going to define a method which is going to return a string, and I'll call the method interpret. And it's going to take another string as an argument, which I'm going to call context. So the context is going to be the variable name I'm checking, which will be a string. And it's going to return the interpreted version of it, which will also be a string. Now, I'm going to implement this for my first rule, which is that there should be no whitespace in the variable name. So I'm going to create another new Java class, and I'm going to call this one NoWhitespace. And this class is going to implement the Expression interface. So I'm going to add implements Expression. And I'm going to override the interpret method. So I'm going to say @Override,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/creating-an-interpreter-for-a-language?u=76281980&t=93)** then I'm going to say public string interpret, and pass in a string called context. This is going to be a terminal operation, which means it will be the last check I do on the variable. So I'm just going to return the context, but with whitespace replaced with an empty string. So I'm going to say return context.replaceAll. And for the first argument, I'm going to put a string with a whitespace. And the second argument is the replacement, so I'm just going to put an empty string. Next, I'm going to make sure that the variable name doesn't begin with a capital letter. So I'm going to create another new class, and I'm going to call this one FirstLetterShouldNotBeLowerCase.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/creating-an-interpreter-for-a-language?u=76281980&t=142)** And again, this is going to implement the Expression interface. So I'm going to add implements Expression. Then I'm going to override the interpret method again. So I'm going to say @Override and public String interpret. And I'm going to pass in a string called context. So this time I'm going to make sure that the first letter of the variable is lowercase. So I'm going to say context equals context.substring and pass in zero for the first character and one for the second character, then I'm going to say .toLowerCase. Then I need to add to the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the string. So I'm going to say + context.substring and pass in one. So this basically says take the first letter of the string, make sure it's lowercase, and then append the rest of the string to that. However, this time I'm not just going to return the context directly. After checking the first letter, I wanted to check for whitespace. So in this class, I'm going to add a new field for NoWhitespace. So I'm going to say private final NoWhitespace noWhitespace equals new NoWhitespace. Then in the interpret method, I'm going to say return noWhitespace.interpret and pass in the new context. Now, I'm going to add one more rule to the chain, which is that the variable name should not start with an underscore character.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/creating-an-interpreter-for-a-language?u=76281980&t=234)** So I'm going to create another new class and I'm going to call it FirstLetterShouldNotBeUnderscore. So again, this is going to implement the Expression interface. And this time this class is going to have a field of type FirstLetterShouldNotBeLowercase. So I'm going to say private final FirstLetterShouldNotBeLowercase firstLetterShouldNotBeLowercase equals new FirstLetterShouldNotBeLowercase. And then I need to override the interpret method again. So I'm going to say @Override, and then public String interpret. And I'm going to pass in a string called context. Then first, I'm going to make sure that the context doesn't start with an underscore. So I'm going to say if context.startsWith and pass in underscore. Then I'm going to say context equals context.substring, one. So that's essentially removing the first character if it's an underscore. Then I'm going to say return firstLetterShouldNotBeLowercase.interpret and pass in the new context. So now I've created an interpreter for the language. As you can see, it would be quite easy to add new rules to the chain, but if there were hundreds of different rules, this would get quite complex. And so I just need to make sure I've spoke return rights in that last line.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-java-design-patterns-creational-behavioral-and-structural/creating-an-interpreter-for-a-language?u=76281980&t=325)** So now I have an interpreter for the language. And now the next step is to actually interpret some variables. So I'll be doing that in the next video.
